# 1300\n
{Cambridge, Trinity College B.14.52: Trinity Homilies, Hand B (all texts in this hand tagged under this number - 1300)}\n
C12b2\n
571 267 E\n
{=W Suffolk=}\n
{=Sample represents all the English in this hand. Hand B writes pp.\n
25-26 (except for 3 words), 28-53 line 15 (end of ORTRO\\wE), 54-58\n
line 5, 59-113, 119-120, 124-127, 134-136, 138, 140-141, 143\n
from word 6-151 word 7, 152-153}\n
{=Script - proto-Gothic book hand=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=Scribe B\'s first contribution is one folio (two pages) of Homily VI\n
which is started and finished by Scribe A - see tagged text no. 1200.\n
The early C16 secretary hand that provided glosses in the first part\n
of the MS continues to be in evidence here, as noted below. The glossed\n
words are usually underlined by the glossing scribe and the glosses\n
interlined above, or in the margin when the word is near the edge of the\n
text block. Sometimes words are underlined, whether by this scribe or\n
some other, without being glossed. The later scribe responsible for\n
the underlining also puts marking sigla of various kinds in the\n
margins. For a full bibliographical description of the manuscript,\n
see Betty Hill, \'Trinity College Cambridge MS. B. 14. 52, and William\n
Patten\', Transactions of the Cambridge Bibliographical Society 4\n
(1966), 192-200 and \'Cambridge, Trinity College MS B.14.52\', TCBS 12\n
(2003), 393-402. Here, in the transcription of the Latin text, punctus\n
and punctus elevatus used as punctuation are separated from the words\n
preceding and following them. Punctus used to indicate abbreviation\n
are here placed immediately after the expansion of the abbreviation,\n
to differentiate this function=}\n
{27}\n

$/P23N_HE\n
{=H apparently corrected from original thorn. Morris (1873)prints\n
yE without reference to the possible correction, and translates\n
the following clause "and then the children are virtuous". This\n
assumes an intransitive use of yEwUEN (see MED s.v. theuen v. 1)\n
which seems not to be recorded elsewhere. Although it is possible\n
that the correction from yE to HE was made by a later corrector and\n
that Morris\'s reading preserves the original and intended form, I\n
think it more likely that Scribe B spotted his syntactic mistake\n
and corrected yE to HE so as to provide a subject referring back\n
to the bishops and priests. Then the sense would be "when they well\n
instruct children". This reading has the advantage of according with\n
the previous and subsequent clause structure in a text notable for\n
extensive syntactic parallelism=}\n
$child/nplOd_CHILDRE\n

{=The U of LIUES is awkwardly written, the first minim being over tall\n

$huely/av_HEU+LICHE $-ly/xs-av_+LICHE\n
{"figuratively"}\n
{=Morris (1873) suggests emendment to HEU [FUL]LICHE taking HEU to mean\n
"you" and to balance the following HEU FREMFULLICHE. The true balance\n
in fact is between "me" and "you" (asking God to guide and help me to\n
expound the meaning and you to understand it). In spite of the\n
coincidental match of its first three letters with the following\n
pronoun (which is in fact not Scribe B\'s normal form for "you" for which\n
elsewhere he has GIU - Scribe A has EU) I believe the word here is\n
more likely to be from OE heow, hiw, in the sense of grammatical\n
"species" or "figura". The adverb here then means "figuratively"=}\n
$to/im+C_TO\n

$gush/vps13_GOSSH+aed $/vps23_+aed\n
{=Morris (1873) prints GOSSHIEd whence the example in MED. But although\n
the aesc is small and the bow of A not very curved, it cannot be for I,\n
which is always given a clear horizontal approach stroke and is never\n
joined to following E. I think that aesc therefore is right. For this\n
ending cf. HABBaed p 43. This verb, if from OScand (cf. OI gusa "gush,\n
spirt out" or gussa "make a fuss, make a noise") would not be expected\n
to have -IEd suffix in the singular. What it means here, though, is\n
doubtful. Morris conjectures "wags" which captures the sense of a wild\n
belly-dance going on, but is hard to justify from the usual meaning of\n
the word in Middle English - "gush out" of liquids, like blood and\n
water. OSS of GOSSaed is underlined and in the right margin is a\n
horizontal stroke and a pointing hand=}\n

$toy?/vi-m_TOI+EN $/vi-m_+EN\n
{=Morris (1873) prints TOTEN, but the I is very clear. OE to:tian means\n
"peep, peek, observe". But here the sense needed is "to take part in, do",\n
(cf. BIHALT. & {LISTEd. & DOd above) because we have the verbs\n
"listen" and "behold" straight after and another "look" verb would be\n
redundant. A verb based on the word "toy" would give perfect sense\n
here: "Each man who comes there to amuse himself with play or to listen\n
to or to behold it..". The only problem is the isolated occurrence of\n
this word at this date. The verb to "toy" is not otherwise recorded\n
before the 16th century. A few examples of the substantive are\n
recorded in MED for later Middle English, the earliest in Robert\n
Manning of Brunne\'s Handlyng Sinne. It is possible, however, that this\n
instance provides an antedating for the verb=}\n
{.}\n

was written. It is hard to tell from microfilm what has happened but the\n
simplest explanation is that Scribe B first wrote wynn after O and then\n
corrected it to U by erasure of the top of the body and addition of\n
outward pointing serifs at the top of the resulting \'minims\' while the\n
descender of the wynn was left to form the \'tail\' of Scribe B\'s usual\n
capital <u>=}\n
